###xml 3215 3219 3167 3171 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 3219 3220 3171 3172 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 3326 3330 3278 3282 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores5">[30]</xref>
###xml 3424 3428 3376 3380 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 3428 3429 3380 3381 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 3516 3520 3468 3472 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 3520 3521 3472 3473 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 3578 3582 3530 3534 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores5">[30]</xref>
###xml 3632 3634 3580 3582 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 3747 3754 3695 3702 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 3802 3804 3746 3748 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 3862 3864 3802 3804 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 3960 3963 3900 3903 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Weber1">[5]</xref>
###xml 4057 4059 3993 3995 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 4098 4101 4034 4037 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Weber1">[5]</xref>
###xml 4103 4106 4039 4042 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Typas1">[6]</xref>
###xml 4108 4112 4044 4048 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Becker1">[21]</xref>
###xml 4283 4285 4215 4217 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
It is important to emphasize that some of the proposed dual promoters have possible poor -10 sigma38 recognition sequences especially those located in pfkAP3-P4, enoP7-P8, pykFP1-P2, pykAP1-P2 and pykAP2-P3 (table 1). Therefore, if any of the putative sigma38 recognition elements that have been described in this report are really not functional in vivo, then an alternative explanation for the decreased RTPCR values in rpoS- derivatives as compared to their rpoS+ parental strains, could be that the transcription of these genes in these PTS- strains must depend indirectly on sigma38. This hypothesis entails that some other factor(s) of the sigma38 regulon could modulate transcription by directly interacting with the transcription machinery or indirectly, allowing differential exposure of certain DNA regions required for transcription. Weber et al[5], have proposed that sigma38 containing RNAp holoenzyme has the ability to cooperate with additional regulatory factors, similar as sigma70 RNAp does. Therefore, some of these regulatory factors that interact with the vegetative sigma70 RNA polymerase could be part of the sigma sigma38 modulon. Hence the absence of one or some of these factors could have a negative impact on the transcription of certain sigma70 promoters. An additional alternative possibility that could also explain, at least partially, lower transcription levels in glycolytic genes in strain PB11rpoS- as compared to PB11 is the fact that the lack of sigma38 may decrease the levels of acetic acid produced by PoxB in these strains, which is used as carbon source through the activity of Acs; this could reduce gluconeogenic and glycolytic fluxes that in turn may decrease both growth rate and the transcription of glycolytic genes in the PB11rpoS- strain. We have published that inactivation of poxB reduced 50% the specific growth rate of PB11poxB- as compared to PB11. We have proposed that less acetate should be produced in the PB11poxB- strain, as well as in the PB11rpoS- strain, since poxB is transcribed by sigma38, to be recycled as a carbon source and therefore this situation could be as mentioned, at least partially, responsible of a decrease in the transcription of glycolytic genes. In fact, transcription of some glycolytic genes is reduced in PB11poxB- when compared to PB11 [30]. It is known that acetate could have a role, directly or indirectly, in the expression of several genes among those coding for regulatory proteins, including those which regulate or belong to the transcription machinery like rpoS and RpoS [3], [11], [64]. Therefore, we compared the expression levels of glycolytic genes in the poxB- and rpoS- derivative strains with the goal of determining the possible roles of sigma38 and acetate in their expression. As mentioned, the transcription values of two of the analyzed glycolytic genes glk and pgi, are reduced roughly at the same levels in the PB11poxB- and PB11rpoS- strains as compared to PB11 [11], [30], allowing the possibility that, low acetate levels could play a role in the expression of at least these two genes. However, since the reported transcription values of other glycolytic genes (aceEF, lpd, pykA, pykF), in the PB11poxB- strain are not modified in comparison with PB11, this result indicates that at least in these five genes [30], lower acetate levels are apparently not responsible of reducing their expression in the PB11rpoS- strain. In addition, the expression of the seven analyzed glycolytic genes in the PB12poxB- strain is not modified in comparison to the PB12 strain [30]. These results support the proposition that sigma38 could play a role in the expression of these genes. Therefore, if these putative dual promoters really function in vivo and if several of them are transcribed by sigma38, this may indicate that transcription initiation by sigma38 is in fact less affected by different deviations from the proposed promoter consensus sequence [5]. Degeneration of the -35 recognition sequences has been associated with the capacity of sigma38 to transcribe "non optimal promoters" [5], [6], [21]. It appears that this could also occur in the -10 region of the promoter because some of the proposed -10 regions in some of the analyzed genes have relatively poor sigma38 recognition sequences.
###end p 90
###begin title 91
###xml 130 132 126 128 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 142 144 134 136 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
Transcription plasticity in central carbon metabolic genes and operons could allow differential expression of these genes by sigma38 and sigma70 subunits
###end title 91
###begin p 92
###xml 980 981 976 977 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1080 1082 1072 1074 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1095 1098 1087 1090 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 1100 1103 1092 1095 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Ueguchi1">[4]</xref>
###xml 1308 1315 1300 1307 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1452 1454 1440 1442 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1734 1737 1722 1725 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Typas1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1739 1743 1727 1731 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Lengeler1">[16]</xref>
###xml 1745 1749 1733 1737 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Grainger1">[24]</xref>
###xml 1751 1755 1739 1743 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Wade1">[27]</xref>
###xml 1837 1840 1825 1828 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dps</italic>
###xml 1890 1893 1878 1881 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dps</italic>
###xml 1937 1939 1921 1923 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1950 1952 1930 1932 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 2146 2148 2122 2124 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 2159 2161 2131 2133 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 2223 2227 2195 2199 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Grainger1">[24]</xref>
###xml 2269 2271 2237 2239 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 2281 2283 2245 2247 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 2428 2432 2392 2396 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 2437 2440 2401 2404 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">acs</italic>
###xml 2727 2729 2687 2689 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 2739 2741 2695 2697 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 3183 3187 3139 3143 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 3527 3530 3483 3486 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">glk</italic>
###xml 3532 3536 3488 3492 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 3538 3542 3494 3498 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">tpiA</italic>
###xml 3544 3548 3500 3504 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykA</italic>
###xml 3640 3642 3592 3594 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 3652 3654 3600 3602 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 3753 3755 3697 3699 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 3766 3769 3710 3713 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Typas1">[6]</xref>
###xml 3771 3775 3715 3719 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Wade1">[27]</xref>
###xml 3827 3829 3767 3769 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 3906 3908 3842 3844 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 3937 3939 3869 3871 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 4136 4140 4068 4072 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Nonaka1">[25]</xref>
###xml 4141 4145 4073 4077 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Wade1">[27]</xref>
###xml 1308 1315 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Transcription plasticity, defined here as the capacity of a gene or an operon to be transcribed by more than one sigma subunit, could be present in all the analyzed genes and operons. This capability could allow differential expression of those genes by the two sigma subunits. Accordingly, in several cases the strength of a specific promoter in one gene was diverse in the various strains with different genetic backgrounds and in different metabolic conditions. Importantly, most of these different sigma possible recognition sites described in this report that could allow transcription plasticity, are superimposed as putative dual promoters. Therefore, transcription plasticity could be present not only at the gene level, but also at the promoter level. Thus, RNAp with different sigma factors holoenzymes could perform transcription, depending on the metabolic conditions and as suggested in this report, as an adaptation response to carbon limitation in cells lacking PTS- growing slowly on glucose in which as mentioned, due to the absence of PTS, higher levels of sigma38 are present [3], [4]. Alarmones, as ppGpp, are involved in modulating sigma subunits affinities for the RNA polymerase core. Therefore, depending on carbon availability that is translated into different ppGpp concentrations, E. coli cells could differentially utilize certain possible dual promoters for the expression of glycolytic genes: less carbon, more sigma sigma38 RNAp holoenzyme. Certainly, other factors such as DksA and Crl, modulate the binding capacities of the different sigma subunits to the RNA polymerase core and also transacting transcriptional regulators such as Fis, IHF and H-NS contribute to promoter plasticity and selectivity [6], [16], [24], [27]. Recently, it has been reported that Fis and H-NS modulate the expression of the dps gene by allowing preferential utilization of the dps promoter a putative dual promoter, by sigma38 over sigma70 when cells reach the stationary phase. These results suggest that the cells can modulate the expression of different promoters in the same gene by allowing the preferential utilization of sigma38 over sigma70 for transcription in cells growing in non optimal conditions [24]. In agreement, the presence of both sigma38 and sigma70 putative dependent promoters in all genes and operons of the glycolytic pathway studied and in other important central metabolism genes such as poxB and acs, could allow the transcription of these central metabolic genes by the cells in both high (optimal) and low (starvation) glucose growing conditions. As mentioned earlier in most cases promoter plasticity could be achieved by selecting superimposed dual promoter sequences for both sigma70 and sigma38 and in other cases, not superimposed promoters could be selected. In the first instance, the cell could have selected this type of superimposed promoters to ensure that transcription initiation is the same in different metabolic conditions; whereas transcription initiation from different promoters could allow a differential postranscriptional regulation. As an example of this case, a new putative dual promoter apparently appeared in the pykF gene in cells lacking PTS. This promoter is preferentially used in these strains in this metabolic node where carbon flux is increased due to the absence of PTS. Importantly, most glycolytic genes are upregulated in PB12 as compared to PB11 as the result of the selection process. As proposed, at least in some of these upregulated genes (glk, gapA, tpiA, pykA) in PB12, higher transcription could be the result of the differential utilization of sigma38 and sigma70. Finally, promoter plasticity could be the result of promoter degeneracy from the vegetative sigma70 sequence. [6], [27]. As previously mentioned, the analysis of the sigma32 regulon revealed an extensive overlap between its targets and those of sigma70, as in the case of the sigma38 regulon. Some degree of promoter degeneration that could be translated into promoter plasticity, probably could allow different RNAp holoenzymes to share transcriptional start sites in many genes [25]-[27].
###end p 92
###begin p 93
###xml 215 218 215 218 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fpb</italic>
###xml 220 224 220 224 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fbaB</italic>
###xml 229 233 229 233 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ppsA</italic>
###xml 233 237 233 237 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Frankel1">[38]</xref>
###xml 309 317 309 317 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g001">figure 1</xref>
###xml 319 322 319 322 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 324 328 324 328 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 365 369 365 369 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapC</italic>
###xml 372 376 372 376 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapC</italic>
###xml 382 386 382 386 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gmpB</italic>
###xml 468 470 464 466 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 536 538 528 530 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 583 586 575 578 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Weber1">[5]</xref>
###xml 588 592 580 584 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 594 638 586 630 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Lacour1">[65, Regulon DB http://www.ccg.unam.mx 2008]</xref>
###xml 678 681 670 673 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fpb</italic>
###xml 683 687 675 679 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fbaB</italic>
###xml 692 696 684 688 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ppsA</italic>
###xml 873 875 861 863 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1012 1015 1000 1003 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">glk</italic>
###xml 1017 1021 1005 1009 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 1023 1027 1011 1015 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykA</italic>
###xml 1032 1037 1020 1025 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">aceEF</italic>
###xml 1126 1129 1114 1117 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pgi</italic>
###xml 1131 1135 1119 1123 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">tpiA</italic>
###xml 1137 1140 1125 1128 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">eno</italic>
###xml 1142 1145 1130 1133 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pgk</italic>
###xml 1173 1175 1157 1159 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1227 1231 1211 1215 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pfkA</italic>
###xml 1233 1237 1217 1221 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 1242 1246 1226 1230 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gpmA</italic>
###xml 1335 1339 1319 1323 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Frankel1">[38]</xref>
Additionally, there are metabolic steps in which both glycolytic and gluconeogenic enzymes coded by different genes are present. In these cases, the expression of the genes coding for gluconeogenic enzymes, such as fpb, fbaB and ppsA[38], was clearly upregulated in strains growing slowly on glucose as PB11 (figure 1) [3], [11]. Also in this strain other genes as gapC1, gapC2 and gmpB were upregulated. As expected, in some of these genes, sequences resembling sigma38 recognition elements have been identified and, accordingly, sigma38 inactivation downregulated their expression [5], [11], [65, Regulon DB http://www.ccg.unam.mx 2008]. Therefore, these genes, together with fpb, fbaB and ppsA that are also upregulated in PB11, could also be involved in the gluconeogenic pathway. Nevertheless, it is important to emphasize that the identification of new possible sigma38 recognition elements reported in this work has been detected in glycolytic genes whose products work only as glycolytic enzymes such as glk, pykF, pykA and aceEF and in genes whose products work in both glycolytic and gluconeogenic reactions such as pgi, tpiA, eno, pgk. Sequences resembling sigma38 recognition sites have been also identified in the pfkA, gapA and gpmA genes whose coded proteins seem to work as well as glycolytic and gluconeogenic enzymes [38].
###end p 93
###begin p 94
###xml 229 230 229 230 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 349 352 348 351 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 506 510 505 509 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 601 605 600 604 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">aceA</italic>
###xml 607 611 606 610 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">aceB</italic>
###xml 613 617 612 616 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">acnB</italic>
###xml 619 623 618 622 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fbaB</italic>
###xml 625 628 624 627 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fbp</italic>
###xml 630 634 629 633 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">glcB</italic>
###xml 636 640 635 639 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapC</italic>
###xml 644 648 643 647 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapC</italic>
###xml 652 656 651 655 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ppsA</italic>
###xml 658 662 657 661 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sfcA</italic>
###xml 673 674 672 673 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 794 798 793 797 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 859 861 854 856 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 871 873 862 864 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
It is possible that most of the described putative dual promoters could also work with gluconeogenic substrates such as acetate. When this compound is used as carbon source simultaneously with glucose in minimal medium, these PTS- strains coutilize both carbon sources due to the lack of catabolite repression with a subsequent increase in their mu [3]. In fact, it has been demonstrated that in addition to those genes mentioned above, which work both as gluconeogenic and glycolytic, the inactivation of rpoS decreases transcription levels of most gluconeogenic and putative gluconeogenic genes as: aceA, aceB, acnB, fbaB, fbp, glcB, gapC-1, gapC-2, ppsA, sfcA in the PTS- strains, indicating a role for this sigma factor in their expression when growing on glucose as the only carbon source [11]. At least in some of these genes, putative overlapping sigma70 and sigma38 possible recognition elements have been detected (unpublished observations).
###end p 94
###begin p 95
###xml 433 436 433 436 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pyk</italic>
Therefore, the presence of both types of putative promoters usually superimposed, could allow the cells the possibility of using different sigma subunits for transcribing a gene in different metabolic conditions. Interestingly, this capacity could be very important, especially in the upper part of the glycolytic pathway, responsible of the transformation of glucose-6P into pyruvate, in which most of the reactions, except for the pyk isoenzymes, are reversible as part of the gluconeogenic pathway. Remarkably, very few transcriptional regulators modulate the expression of this group of glycolytic genes, as discussed in the next section.
###end p 95
###begin title 96
Expression of the genes involved in the transformation of glucose-6P into pyruvate seems not to be substantially regulated by general transcription regulatory elements. The presence of different promoters in these genes could allow a permanent transcription response in different metabolic conditions
###end title 96
###begin p 97
###xml 341 346 341 346 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ptsHI</italic>
###xml 348 352 348 352 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pfkA</italic>
###xml 354 358 354 358 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 368 380 368 380 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">epd-pgk-fbaA</italic>
###xml 452 459 452 459 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 753 760 753 760 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 760 764 760 764 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Saier2">[66]</xref>
###xml 766 770 766 770 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Perrenoud1">[67]</xref>
###xml 807 814 807 814 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 999 1002 999 1002 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cra</italic>
###xml 1443 1447 1443 1447 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Bledig1">[49]</xref>
###xml 1449 1453 1449 1453 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Saier2">[66]</xref>
###xml 1455 1459 1455 1459 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Perrenoud1">[67]</xref>
###xml 1752 1755 1752 1755 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">glk</italic>
###xml 1760 1763 1760 1763 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pgi</italic>
###xml 1845 1849 1845 1849 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pfkA</italic>
###xml 1854 1858 1854 1858 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 1873 1885 1873 1885 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">epd-pgk-fbaA</italic>
###xml 2173 2177 2173 2177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 2182 2194 2182 2194 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">epd-pgk-fbaA</italic>
###xml 2220 2221 2220 2221 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 2349 2353 2349 2353 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Saier2">[66]</xref>
###xml 2355 2359 2355 2359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Perrenoud1">[67]</xref>
###xml 2422 2426 2422 2426 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 2445 2457 2445 2457 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">epd-gmk-fbaA</italic>
###xml 2493 2497 2493 2497 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 2606 2610 2606 2610 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 2661 2665 2661 2665 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Charpentier1">[41]</xref>
###xml 2667 2671 2667 2671 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Charpentier2">[42]</xref>
###xml 2711 2715 2711 2715 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gapA</italic>
###xml 2728 2732 2728 2732 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pykF</italic>
###xml 2741 2753 2741 2753 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">epd-pgk-fbaA</italic>
###xml 2870 2876 2870 2876 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E coli</italic>
###xml 2995 2998 2995 2998 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Ferenci1">[7]</xref>
###xml 3000 3003 3000 3003 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Hua1">[8]</xref>
###xml 3005 3009 3005 3009 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Death1">[12]</xref>
###xml 3011 3015 3011 3015 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Ferenci2">[15]</xref>
###xml 3312 3313 3312 3313 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 3698 3705 3698 3705 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 452 459 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 753 760 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 807 814 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 3698 3705 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Remarkably, few general transcription regulators have been reported to be involved in modulating the expression of most glycolytic genes -Cra and Crp, among others- especially at the upper part of the glycolytic pathway involved in the transformation of glucose-6P into pyruvate. Cra works as a repressor of certain glycolytic genes such as ptsHI, pfkA, pykF, and the epd-pgk-fbaA operon and as an activator of some gluconeogenic genes. When wild type E. coli grows fast aerobically on glucose, such as strain JM101, Cra whose DNA binding activity is modulated by sugar catabolites (fructose-1-P and fructose-1-6P), does not significantly repress glycolytic genes. This explains why Cra inactivation exerts no effect on the growth rate of the wild type E. coli[66], [67]. In gluconeogenic conditions, where E. coli grows relatively slow, transcription of glycolytic Cra regulated genes should be decreased, while transcription of Cra regulated gluconeogenic genes should be increased; in agreement, cra inactivation inhibits growth on gluconeogenic substrates. These results can be explained by the fact that sugar catabolites present at higher concentrations when growing rapidly on glucose, bind to Cra and displace it from the operator sites in target genes. However, in gluconeogenic conditions these sugar catabolites are present in relatively lower concentrations thus allowing Cra to repress glycolytic and activate gluconeogenic genes [49], [66], [67]. In strain PB11 that grows slowly on glucose sharing some responses of the gluconeogenic metabolism, particularly the fact that sugar catabolites are probably in lower concentrations as compared to the wild type, the transcription of glycolytic genes was downregulated (with the exception of glk and pgi), as compared to JM101. The possible role of Cra in modulating the expression of pfkA and pykF genes and the epd-pgk-fbaA operon downregulated in PB11, could be part of a more general mechanism, in which due to the absence of PTS, not only the Cra regulated genes but most glycolytic genes were downregulated in PB11. Nevertheless, downregulation of these genes may be due in part to Cra repression; however, pykF and epd-pgk-fbaA transcription in both PTS- strains was only slightly downregulated as compared to JM101, suggesting, a minor role of Cra in the expression of these genes [66], [67] in the tested conditions. Crp regulates the expression of the gapA gene and also the epd-gmk-fbaA operon. However, the expression of gapA in these strains in these growing conditions with glucose as the only carbon source, occurred only from the gapAP1 reported promoter which is not regulated by Crp [41], [42]. Therefore, neither Crp in the case of gapA nor Cra for pykF and the epd-pgk-fbaA operon were apparently significantly involved in modulating the expression of these genes in the tested conditions. E coli in its natural environment must rapidly adjust its metabolism from conditions with high to low glucose concentrations [7], [8], [12], [15]. The glycolytic pathway, from glucose-6P to pyruvate, is a flexible pathway in which, as mentioned, most of the steps work in both directions, allowing the utilization of gluconeogenic substrates to produce glucose-6P. In fact, the glycolytic and gluconeogenic pathways work simultaneously in PTS- strains that grow slowly on glucose. The apparent absence of regulation at the level of transcription of most glycolytic genes involved in the transformation of glucose-6P into pyruvate, except the ones mentioned above, may indicate that the modulation of the expression of the genes coding for this critical reversible pathway is being performed by a different strategy. Apparently, E. coli could have selected a less transcriptional regulated glycolytic pathway at least in the initial part of the pathway, in which typical repressors or activators are less involved, in order to allow permanent transcription of the genes involved in these reactions in different metabolic conditions. Transcription plasticity and promoter redundancy, utilizing several sigma subunits, could allow a more flexible transcriptional regulatory strategy for this section of the glycolytic pathway, in which several reactions may also be gluconeogenic.
###end p 97
###begin title 98
###xml 17 19 13 15 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
The role of sigma38 in the transformation of pyruvate into AcCoA in different growing conditions: carbon flux divertion during carbon limitation
###end title 98
###begin p 99
###xml 493 495 489 491 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 495 498 491 494 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Weber1">[5]</xref>
###xml 500 504 496 500 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 506 510 502 506 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Quail1">[51]</xref>
###xml 531 533 523 525 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 543 545 531 533 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 624 628 612 616 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pdhR</italic>
###xml 702 709 690 697 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 827 835 815 823 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g001">figure 1</xref>
###xml 921 925 909 913 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 925 926 913 914 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1014 1019 1000 1005 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">aceEF</italic>
###xml 1061 1065 1047 1051 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 1070 1073 1056 1059 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">acs</italic>
###xml 1191 1192 1177 1178 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1201 1204 1187 1190 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 1206 1210 1192 1196 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 1212 1216 1198 1202 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores4">[29]</xref>
###xml 1218 1222 1204 1208 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores5">[30]</xref>
###xml 1224 1228 1210 1214 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-AbdelHamid1">[55]</xref>
###xml 1260 1264 1246 1250 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pdhR</italic>
###xml 1293 1301 1279 1287 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g001">figure 1</xref>
###xml 1396 1399 1382 1385 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Weber1">[5]</xref>
###xml 1565 1569 1551 1555 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-McFall1">[62]</xref>
###xml 1674 1678 1659 1663 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Sigala1">[64]</xref>
###xml 1743 1747 1728 1732 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 1747 1748 1732 1733 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1841 1848 1825 1832 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0007466-t002">table 2</xref>
###xml 2173 2187 2156 2170 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pdhR-aceEF-lpd</italic>
###xml 2231 2235 2214 2218 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Quail1">[51]</xref>
###xml 2280 2284 2263 2267 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 2284 2285 2267 2268 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 2312 2314 2291 2293 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 2866 2870 2843 2847 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 2870 2871 2847 2848 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 3089 3096 3066 3073 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 702 709 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 3089 3096 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Nevertheless, there are some critical metabolic steps of the central carbon metabolic pathway that are clearly transcriptionally regulated, such as the transformation of pyruvate into AcCoA performed by both Pdh and PoxB/Acs in different growing conditions. As mentioned, in the transformation of pyruvate into AcCoA, PdhR works as a specific transcriptional repressor of the Pdh operon when pyruvate is present at low concentrations; its expression is controlled by Crp and regulated by sigma38[5], [11], [51]. In agreement, sigma70 and sigma38 superimposed overlapping putative promoters were reported in this work in the pdhR gene. In conditions where bacterial cells are growing slowly on glucose, E. coli apparently utilizes PoxB for pyruvate oxidation into acetate, reducing the role of Pdh for pyruvate decarboxylation (figure 1). These propositions are supported by the facts that PoxB inactivation in strain PB11poxB- reduced mu by 50% whereas in JM101 the effect was minor (5% reduction in mu). In PB11, aceEF transcription values were reduced, while poxB and acs were upregulated as compared to JM101. Additionally, the carbon flux between pyruvate and AcCoA is doubled in the PTS- strains [3], [11], [29], [30], [55]. Importantly, transcription of pdhR is not upregulated in PB11 (figure 1), but is upregulated in other stress conditions in a wild type strain as previously mentioned [5]. Furthermore, it has been demonstrated that in cultures with acetate as the only carbon source, the addition of alanine which can rapidly be converted into pyruvate [62], increases the mu of strains PB11 and PB12, indicating intrinsic pyruvate deficiencies in these strains [64]. In fact, a similar effect was observed in strains PB11 and PB11rpoS- growing on glucose. As mentioned, the addition of alanine increased their mu substantially (table 2). These results are in agreement with the proposition that in strain PB11, in which low pyruvate concentrations are apparently present, the addition of alanine, increased its mu 100%, because in these conditions more pyruvate is probably utilized by Pdh instead of PoxB due to the lack of repression of the transcription of pdhR-aceEF-lpd operon by PdhR in the presence of pyruvate [51]. This effect is more dramatic in strain PB11rpoS- where the absence of sigma38, and consequently PoxB, has an important detrimental effect on the mu, since no carbon can be recycled through PoxB/Acs. In this strain, the addition of alanine increased mu in 250%, reaching a specific growth rate, only 25% lower than PB11, indicating again that in these conditions with high pyruvate concentrations due to the addition of alanine, PB11 utilizes preferentially the Pdh complex, reducing the role of PoxB, to convert pyruvate into AcCoA. Interestingly, no effect was detected when alanine was added to cultures of strain PB12 and PB12rpoS-, in agreement with the higher glycolytic flux in PB12 where pyruvate should be present in higher concentrations. These results support the metabolic carbon flux diversion proposed at the pyruvate node. In conclusion, E. coli has two alternative routes to produce AcCoA from pyruvate that are differentially utilized in distinct growing conditions.
###end p 99
###begin p 100
###xml 145 152 145 152 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 205 209 205 209 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Moreau1">[68]</xref>
###xml 228 232 228 232 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">soxS</italic>
###xml 471 473 467 469 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 607 611 603 607 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pdhR</italic>
###xml 758 762 754 758 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 970 971 966 967 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1169 1171 1161 1163 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 1181 1183 1169 1171 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1233 1237 1221 1225 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 1242 1245 1230 1233 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">acs</italic>
###xml 1500 1508 1488 1496 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g001">figure 1</xref>
###xml 1510 1513 1498 1501 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 1515 1518 1503 1506 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Ferenci1">[7]</xref>
###xml 1520 1523 1508 1511 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Hua1">[8]</xref>
###xml 1525 1529 1513 1517 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 1531 1535 1519 1523 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores3">[28]</xref>
###xml 1536 1540 1524 1528 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores5">[30]</xref>
###xml 1542 1546 1530 1534 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Wise1">[57]</xref>
###xml 1548 1552 1536 1540 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Sigala1">[64]</xref>
###xml 1554 1558 1542 1546 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Moreau1">[68]</xref>
###xml 1560 1564 1548 1552 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Wolfe1">[69]</xref>
###xml 145 152 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Diversion of metabolic flux from Pdh to PoxB is also apparently involved in decreasing oxidative stress during glucose metabolism in non growing E. coli cells under aerobic phosphate starvation conditions [68]. In fact in PB11, soxS is upregulated as compared to JM101 (unpublished results). Therefore, it appears that this carbon flux diversion is a more general strategy to deal with different stress conditions including slow growth on glucose. We emphasize that sigma38 is directly involved during growth on non optimal conditions, in two aspects: 1) in the regulation, possibly by transcription of the pdhR gene whose product decrease the expression of the Pdh operon in the absence of pyruvate, and, 2) in the transcription that allows upregulation of poxB, which allowed carbon flux diversion, during carbon limitation, from Pdh to PoxB. This metabolic adaptation may allow cells growing slowly on glucose the possibility of preserving carbon atoms by reducing CO2 production at the TCA, probably by recycling higher amounts of AcCoA into the glyoxalate shunt and the gluconeogenic pathways for carbon preservation purposes. Remarkably, strong superimposed sigma70 and sigma38 putative dual promoters were also located in the poxB and acs genes that code for the enzymes that perform this important "Pox-Acs-shunt" involved in the transformation of pyruvate into acetate, with concomitant generation of reducing power at the membrane and the recycling and incorporation of acetate into AcCoA (figure 1) [3], [7], [8], [11], [28]-[30], [57], [64], [68], [69].
###end p 100
###begin title 101
Comments on the differential expression of some of these genes in the evolved strain PB12 as compared to PB11
###end title 101
###begin p 102
###xml 503 507 503 507 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Sigala1">[64]</xref>
###xml 580 582 576 578 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 592 594 584 586 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">70</sup>
###xml 789 793 781 785 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 980 984 972 976 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 984 985 976 977 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1050 1052 1038 1040 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">38</sup>
###xml 1104 1112 1092 1100 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g001">figure 1</xref>
###xml 1185 1188 1173 1176 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">gpp</italic>
###xml 1190 1194 1178 1182 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">spoT</italic>
###xml 1196 1199 1184 1187 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndk</italic>
###xml 1201 1204 1189 1192 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ppa</italic>
###xml 1436 1440 1424 1428 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
During the selection process performed to isolate PB12, several mutations occurred and one (or more) of them allowed the upregulation of glycolytic genes that permitted faster growth on glucose (3, 11, unpublished results). However, the expression of these genes remained upregulated in a gluconeogenic substrate as acetate, suggesting that this general mechanism which has been modified is responsible for the upregulation of the glycolytic genes in both glycolytic and gluconeogenic growth conditions [64]. In addition, as mentioned, a possible differential utilization by sigma38 and sigma70 of the proposed putative promoters in these genes, could also play a relevant role in their differential expression in these genes in these strains. Interestingly, as mentioned, inactivation of rpoS clearly has not an important effect on the growth rate of PB12, as compared to PB11. In agreement, RTPCR values of many glycolytic genes in PB12 are not substantially reduced in the PB12rpoS- derivative as compared to PB12, indicating a minor role of sigma38 in the regulation of many of the glycolytic genes (figure 1). Remarkably, in this strain there is upregulation (3.5x) of the genes (gpp, spoT, ndk, ppa) involved in ppGpp metabolism. This situation could modify, probably reducing, the concentrations of this alarmone in PB12 as compared to PB11, and in turn could allow a differential utilization of these sigma subunits by the RNAp [11].
###end p 102
###begin p 103
###xml 103 110 103 110 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 173 174 173 174 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 103 110 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
The analysis of the mutations that occurred in PB12 will allow a better insight into the physiology of E. coli strains that grows permanently stressed on glucose such as PTS- strains and as well as a deeper understanding on the metabolic plasticity of this bacteria that has the capability of generating new metabolic adaptive capabilities in the absence of the general regulatory PTS.
###end p 103
###begin title 104
Materials and Methods
###end title 104
###begin title 105
1) Bacterial strains, media and growth conditions
###end title 105
###begin p 106
###xml 0 7 0 7 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 33 37 29 33 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ptsH</italic>
###xml 39 43 35 39 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ptsI</italic>
###xml 45 48 41 44 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">crr</italic>
###xml 51 54 47 50 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">kan</italic>
###xml 59 60 55 56 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 91 95 83 87 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ptsH</italic>
###xml 97 101 89 93 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ptsI</italic>
###xml 103 106 95 98 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">crr</italic>
###xml 108 111 100 103 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">kan</italic>
###xml 119 120 111 112 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 159 162 151 154 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">tra</italic>
###xml 170 171 162 163 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 176 177 168 169 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 166 181 158 173 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">proA<sup>+</sup> proB<sup>+</sup> lac</italic>
###xml 182 183 174 175 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">q</sup>
###xml 190 193 178 181 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">lac</italic>
###xml 199 207 187 195 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">supE thi</italic>
###xml 214 217 198 201 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">lac</italic>
###xml 218 223 202 207 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">proAB</italic>
###xml 226 230 210 214 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores3">[28]</xref>
###xml 232 236 216 220 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Bolvar1">[70]</xref>
###xml 276 280 260 264 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 343 347 327 331 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 351 352 335 336 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 365 369 349 353 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 373 374 357 358 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 374 378 358 362 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
###xml 557 564 541 548 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 682 687 666 671 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al</italic>
###xml 1059 1062 1037 1040 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 0 7 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 557 564 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
E. coli strain PB12 (JM101 Delta(ptsH, ptsI, crr)::kan, Glc+) was obtained from PB11 Delta(ptsH, ptsI, crr::kan), a PTS- mutant derivative of strain JM101 (F' traD36 proA+ proB+ lacIq Delta(lacZ)Z15/supE thi Delta(lac-proAB)) [28], [70]). Derivatives of these strains lacking rpoS were previously obtained by P1 transduction using strain RH90 rpoS::TCr as donor of rpoS::Tcr[11]. These strains were utilized for isolating RNA that was previously used for RTPCR transcription experiments; the same RNA was used for the modified 5'RACE methodology. Wild type E. coli strain MG1665, was used for the extraction of RNA that was utilized for the global transcription experiment (Mendoza et al, submitted to PLoS ONE). Duplicate cultures for RNA isolation, were grown aerobically in 1 l fermentors with 700 ml of M9 minimal medium with 2g/l of glucose as the only carbon source, at 37degreesC, 600 rpm controlling pH at 7 with NaOH and air flow rate of 1 vvm. Bacterial growth was monitored and cells were collected at 1.0 (OD 600 nm) when growing in the log phase [3]. Strain Top 10 (Invitrogen) was used as the recipient of DNA fusions carrying specific promoters with a reporter gene.
###end p 106
###begin title 107
###xml 146 147 146 147 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">r</sup>
###xml 94 97 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
2) Construction of DNA fusions carrying promoters of several glycolytic genes with a reporter cat gene that confers chloramphenicol resistance (Cmr)
###end title 107
###begin p 108
###xml 124 127 124 127 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pgi</italic>
###xml 129 133 129 133 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pfkA</italic>
###xml 135 139 135 139 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">tpiA</italic>
###xml 141 144 141 144 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pgk</italic>
###xml 150 153 150 153 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">eno</italic>
###xml 181 184 181 184 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cat</italic>
###xml 224 228 224 228 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-SnchezSanMartin1">[71]</xref>
###xml 526 534 526 534 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s004">table S1</xref>
###xml 747 751 747 751 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SmaI</italic>
###xml 808 811 808 811 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cat</italic>
###xml 843 852 843 852 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s003">figure S3</xref>
###xml 854 858 854 858 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-SnchezSanMartin1">[71]</xref>
###xml 905 912 905 912 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1117 1121 1113 1117 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PstI</italic>
###xml 1241 1250 1237 1246 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s003">figure S3</xref>
###xml 181 184 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
###xml 808 811 <span type="species:ncbi:9685">cat</span>
###xml 905 912 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
DNA fusions carrying different chromosomal DNA regions with the "closest to the ATG promoters" of various glycolytic genes (pgi, pfkA, tpiA, pgk, and eno), were constructed using a cat reporter gene, as previously described [71]. Briefly, chromosomal DNA regions were constructed carrying the promoters of the selected genes and the first 20 bp of the specific structural gene. The nucleotide sequences of the oligonucleotides that were utilized for amplifying the selected specific DNA chromosomal fragments are presented in table S1. In all cases two oligos were used for isolating the closest to the ATG promoter present in these genes: one forward (Fw) and one reverse (Rv). Each different fragment was amplified, and the product ligated into SmaI digested pKK232-8; this site is located in front of the cat reporter gene in this plasmid (figure S3) [71]. Recombinant molecules were used to transform E. coli strain Top 10. Transformed cells were plated on minimal medium with glucose and chloramphenicol (10 microg/ml). Alkaline minipreps of different construction were performed and plasmids were digested with PstI to verify the size of the ligated chromosomal DNA. The nucleotide sequence of the cloned inserts, was also determined (figure S3).
###end p 108
###begin title 109
3) RNA Extraction
###end title 109
###begin p 110
###xml 159 160 159 160 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">g</italic>
###xml 428 431 413 416 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores1">[3]</xref>
###xml 433 437 418 422 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Flores2">[11]</xref>
Total RNA extraction was performed using a hot phenol method. After extractions, RNA was precipitated with 3 M sodium acetate/ethanol and centrifuged at 20,000g at 4degreesC. Supernatant was discarded and the RNA resuspended in water and treated with Dnase kit (DNA-freetrade mark, Ambion, USA). RNA concentrations were determined by absorbance at 260/280 nm ratio. RNA integrity was verified by electrophoresis in agarose gels [3], [11].
###end p 110
###begin title 111
4) Construction of cDNA libraries using 5'RACE for TSSs mapping
###end title 111
###begin p 112
###xml 44 48 44 48 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">poxB</italic>
###xml 53 56 53 56 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">acs</italic>
###xml 94 98 94 98 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Fromhman1">[72]</xref>
###xml 100 104 100 104 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0007466-Schaefer1">[73]</xref>
###xml 208 213 208 213 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al</italic>
###xml 293 300 293 300 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 329 333 329 333 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rpoS</italic>
###xml 333 334 333 334 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 495 523 495 523 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GCCTTGCCAGCCCGCTCANNNNNN3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 293 300 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
For determining the TSSs of the glycolytic, poxB and acs genes we used the 5'RACE methodology [72], [73] with some important modifications. A more detailed protocol is described in a separate report (Mendoza et al, submitted to PLoS ONE). Briefly, we generated cDNA libraries for each strain (E. coli JM101, PB11, PB12 and their rpoS- derivatives) by reverse transcription using SuperScript III reverse transcriptase (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, USA) together with an hexamer random primer-adaptor B (5'GCCTTGCCAGCCCGCTCANNNNNN3'). The cDNA synthesis reactions were incubated in a RoboCycler equipment (Stratagene, Amsterdan, The Netherlands) under the following program (28degreesC for 20 min, 45degreesC for 40 min, 70degreesC for 10 min). The cDNA final products were purified using the High Pure PCR product purification kit (Roche Indianapolis, USA), according to the manufacture's instructions. Purified cDNA libraries were labeled (tagged) at the 3' terminal end using two approaches. In the first one, the 3' terminal end was labeled with a tail of adenine polynucleotide using a terminal transferase reaction. In this case, 20 microl of purified cDNA sample was mixed with terminal deoxynucleotidyl transferase (20 U/microl), dATP 0.2 mM final concentration and 1x reaction buffer in a final reaction volume of 25 microl. The reaction was incubated at 37degreesC for 30 min, followed by enzyme inactivation at 70degreesC for 10 min.
###end p 112
###begin p 113
###xml 108 137 108 137 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAGNNNNNN3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 137 160 137 160 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">3&#8242;CGGAGGGAGCGCGGTAGTC5&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 658 665 634 641 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0007466-t001">table 1</xref>
In the second approach, the 3' terminal end was labeled with the adaptor A, a double stranded DNA sequence (5'GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAGNNNNNN3'3'CGGAGGGAGCGCGGTAGTC5') by ligation. In this type of experiment, 5 microl of purified cDNA sample was mixed with T4 DNA ligase (1 Weiss U/microl), 1x reaction buffer, 35 pmol of adaptor A, in a final reaction volume of 25 microl. The reaction was incubated at 16degreesC overnight, following by enzyme inactivation at 70degreesC for 10 min. In most cases only one of these two approaches was utilized for determine the TSSs; however, there were some genes in which both approaches were utilized for TSSs determinations (table 1). All the reagents were purchased from Fermentas (St. Leon-Rot, Germany).
###end p 113
###begin title 114
5) Linear or exponential amplification of tagged cDNA libraries
###end title 114
###begin p 115
###xml 165 204 165 204 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GACTCGAGTCGACATCGATTTTTTTTTTTTTTTTT3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 796 819 766 789 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAG3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 828 849 798 819 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GCCTTGCCAGCCCGCTC3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 1232 1241 1172 1181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g002">Figures 2</xref>
###xml 1243 1244 1183 1184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g003">3</xref>
###xml 1246 1247 1186 1187 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0007466-g004">4</xref>
###xml 1252 1254 1192 1194 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s001">S1</xref>
As a first approach we performed a linear PCR amplification in order to enrich the yield of the tagged cDNA complementary strand. Briefly, 20 pmol of RACE 1 primer (5'GACTCGAGTCGACATCGATTTTTTTTTTTTTTTTT3') which harbors an adaptor sequence at its 5' terminal end, was allowed to anneal to the poly A tract of the tagged cDNA, and used to linearly expand the library in an standard PCR amplification reaction under the following conditions: 1 cycle, 94degreesC for 10 min; 30 cycles of 94degreesC for 1 min, 45degreesC for 2 min, 72degreesC for 3 min, and finally one last extension cycle at 72degreesC for 5 min). In the second approach we performed exponential amplifications of the tagged cDNA libraries. Briefly, cDNA with labeled terminal ends was used as template and PCR primer adaptors A (5'GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAG3') and B (5'GCCTTGCCAGCCCGCTC3') for a PCR amplification reaction under the following conditions: 1 cycle, 94degreesC for 2 min; 35 cycles of 94degreesC for 1 min, 59degreesC for 45 sec and 72degreesC for 45 sec; 1 cycle, 72degreesC for 2 min. Both types of amplified samples were purified using the High Pure PCR product purification kit (Roche, Indianapolis, USA), accordingly to the manufacturers instructions. Figures 2, 3, 4 and S1 show the PAGE results of the amplified fragments.
###end p 115
###begin title 116
6) Prime-specific PCR amplification and nucleotide sequence determination for TSSs identification
###end title 116
###begin p 117
###xml 279 303 279 303 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GACTCGAGTCGACATCGATT3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 308 331 308 331 <named-content xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" content-type="gene">5&#8242;GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAG3&#8242;</named-content>
###xml 513 521 513 521 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s004">table S1</xref>
###xml 993 1000 993 1000 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1231 1240 1231 1240 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0007466.s001">figure S1</xref>
###xml 993 1005 <span type="species:ncbi:83333">E. coli K-12</span>
Both cDNA pools the linear and the exponentially amplified, were used as templates to selectively and individually amplify each selected gene using the high fidelity PCR system (Fast Start High Fidelity PCR System, Roche USA). This was achieved by using general adaptor primers (5'GACTCGAGTCGACATCGATT3' and 5'GCCTCCCTCGCGCCATCAG3') and a primer that specifically anneals to the cDNA complementary strand of the target gene. The nucleotide sequences of the specific primers of the analyzed genes are presented in table S1. A sample of the PCR product was analyzed by 8% native polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis and the band or bands, according to the number of detected TSSs, were then excised and purified from the gel. Finally, the nucleotide sequences of purified PCR products were determined using the same specific gene-primer employed for PCR amplification. Sequence reactions were run in an Applied Biosystem 3100 Genetic Analyzer/ABI PRISM device. The sequences were aligned with the E. coli K-12 genome of strain MG1655 and the specific TSSs, were identified as the first nucleotide immediately adjacent to the utilized adaptor. The nucleotide sequences obtained for the different genes and various TSSs are presented in figure S1.
###end p 117
###begin title 118
7) Transcription start sites determination by pyrosequencing
###end title 118
###begin p 119
###xml 66 71 66 71 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">et al</italic>
###xml 223 230 223 230 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0007466-t001">table 1</xref>
###xml 256 270 256 270 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli strain</italic>
###xml 256 263 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Using random primers and the methodology described in the Mendoza et al (submitted to PLoS ONE), we were able to determine the TSSs of twelve promoters of glycolytic genes in wild type strain MG1665. These are presented in table 1. Briefly, total RNA from E. coli strain MG1665 grown in LB and minimal media at both 37degrees and 30degreesC was extracted and the rRNA was eliminated. For each sample a cDNA library was prepared utilizing the double adapter method (A and B previously described in 4). Using primers complementary to adaptors A and B, PCR amplicons were generated by Fast Start High Fidelity PCR System (Roche Applied Sciences, Indianapolis, USA) purified with MiniElute PCR purification Kit (Qiagen, Valencia, USA), and quantified using the NanoDrop spectrophotometer (NanoDrop Technologies, Wilmington, USA). At least 3 ug of cDNA were obtained for each sample. The quality of the DNA was evaluated by capillar electrophoresis using the Agilent Bioanalyser 2100 (Agilent Technologies, Palo Alto, USA). For pyrosequencing, samples were prepared according to 454 Roche GS FLX DNA Amplicon Library Preparation Kit user manual. Each amplicon mix was sequenced independently using the GS emPCR Kit II (454 Life Sciences Corporation, Branford, USA).
###end p 119
